Let’s define:

x_P = (d + R - r)/2.

We have C = (-r, 0). So distance squared from P to C: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

So we don't need to enforce equality, it's already satisfied by that x-coordinate. Then we need P to be equidistant to A and C (or A and D). So set |P A|^2 = |P C|^2.

Compute |P C|^2: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2. But note that x_P + r = (d + R - r)/2 + r = (d + R - r + 2r)/2 = (d + R + r)/2. So that's (d + R + r)/2.

Similarly, |P D|^2: (x_P - (d+R))^2 + y_P^2. x_P - (d+R) = (d + R - r)/2 - (d+R) = (d + R - r - 2d - 2R)/2 = (-d - R - r)/2 = -(d + R + r)/2. Squared gives same. So indeed equal.

Now compute |P A|^2: (x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2. Set equal to (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Thus:

(x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

(x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2) + (y_P^2 - 2h y_P + h^2) = (x_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2) + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2:

-2a x_P + a^2 - 2h y_P + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Bring terms:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2 (since A is on Ω). So a^2 + h^2 = r^2. Then:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel r^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P = 2r x_P.

Divide 2:

- a x_P - h y_P = r x_P.

Thus:

- h y_P = r x_P + a x_P = x_P (r + a).

So:

y_P = - (x_P (r + a)) / h.

That's a much simpler expression! Let's double-check the algebra.

We had:

(x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Compute:

(x_P - a)^2 = x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2.
(y_P - h)^2 = y_P^2 - 2h y_P + h^2.
Sum = x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2.

Right side: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2 + y_P^2.

Set equal:

x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel x_P^2 + y_P^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2. So:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel r^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P = 2r x_P.

Divide by 2:

- a x_P - h y_P = r x_P.

Thus:

- h y_P = r x_P + a x_P = x_P (r + a).

So y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h.

Given the difficulty, an analytic approach seems plausible. Let's attempt to compute everything symbolically and simplify.

We have parameters: d, r, R. But note that a and h are expressed in terms of d, r, R. Also we have r < R, and circles intersect, so |R - r| < d < R + r. That's all.

We need y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h. Let's compute a and h^2, then r + a and simplify.

Define:

a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d).

Then r + a = r + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = (2dr + d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d).

That's interesting. Similarly, we might compute h^2 = r^2 - a^2.

Now, h appears in denominator. We can also express h^2 in terms of d, r, R.

Compute a^2:

a^2 = ( (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) / (4d^2).

Then h^2 = r^2 - a^2 = (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4d^2). That's a common expression (related to the area of triangle formed by centers and intersection). That's the squared distance from the radical axis? Actually it's the square of the half-length of the common chord. So h = (1/(2d)) * sqrt( ( (R + r)^2 - d^2) * (d^2 - (R - r)^2 ) )? There is formula for common chord length: AB = (1/d) * sqrt( ( (R + r)^2 - d^2) * (d^2 - (R - r)^2) ) ). That's the length of the common chord. Then half-length h = AB/2. But we can compute h^2 directly. Let's derive:

We have h^2 = r^2 - a^2, where a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So:

4d^2 h^2 = 4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2.

That's a factorization: (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) = ( (2dr)^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) = (2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)) * (2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)). Let's compute:

2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = -d^2 - r^2 + 2dr + R^2 = R^2 - (d^2 - 2dr + r^2) = R^2 - (d - r)^2.

Similarly, 2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2 = (d + r)^2 - R^2.

Thus:

4d^2 h^2 = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) * ((d + r)^2 - R^2).

Since circles intersect, both factors are positive: R^2 - (d - r)^2 > 0 (because d > |R - r|) and (d + r)^2 - R^2 > 0 (because d < R + r). So indeed positive.

Thus h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4d^2}.

We also have (r + a) expression: (r + a) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d). So indeed (r + a) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d). That's positive.

That looks messy. But perhaps we can simplify further using the expression for h. Since h^2 involves ((d + r)^2 - R^2) and (R^2 - (d - r)^2). So (r + a) is proportional to sqrt of that factor. And y_P is something like - (x_P * (r + a)) / h. That's a rational expression.

The power relation is: For point P, if we draw a line intersecting Ω at points U and V, then (PU)*(PV) = power of P wrt Ω (with sign if directed). So if we take line AP, then the intersection points are A and E, so (PA)*(PE) = power(P, Ω) = |PM|^2 - r^2. Thus we can avoid solving quadratic and get E and F expressed in terms of P, A, and the circles' powers. However we need to be careful about sign: PA is directed distance along line AP, but if we treat it as signed, then PA * PE = power. Since we have A and E are two intersections, and P is on the line, the signed distances from P to A and to E (in the same direction) multiply to power. If P lies outside the segment AE, then both signed distances have same sign, product positive; if P lies between A and E, then they have opposite signs, product negative. The power can be negative if P is inside the circle. So we can use that.

Simplify (r + a)^2 / h^2 = \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }. So

|P|^2 - r^2 = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }\right) - r^2.

Thus |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{4 d r x_P^2}{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } - r^2.

Now, plug x_P^2 = \frac{ (d + R - r)^2 }{4}. So

|P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{4 d r}{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } * \frac{ (d + R - r)^2 }{4} - r^2 = \frac{ d r (d + R - r)^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } - r^2.

Now, we can also compute |v|^2 using the expression |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. So

|v|^2 = (|P|^2 - r^2) + r^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2 = (|P|^2 - r^2) + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2.

But careful: |P|^2 = (|P|^2 - r^2) + r^2. So |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2 = (|P|^2 - r^2) + r^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2 = (|P|^2 - r^2) + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2. That's okay.

Thus |v|^2 = \frac{ d r (d + R - r)^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } - r^2 + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2.

Simplify - r^2 + 2 r^2 = r^2. So

|v|^2 = \frac{ d r (d + R - r)^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Now, x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So 2 r x_P = r (d + R - r). So

That seems messy, but perhaps it simplifies nicely. Let's compute the fraction.

We have denominator: R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - (d - r))(R + (d - r)). But maybe we can relate (d + R - r)^2 and R^2 - (d - r)^2. Compute:

(d + R - r)^2 = (R + (d - r))^2? Actually d + R - r = (R + d) - r. That's not directly. But note that (d + R - r) and (R - d + r) are related. We have (d + R - r) * (R - d + r) = (R + d - r)*(R - d + r) = ((R) + (d - r)) * ((R) - (d - r)) = R^2 - (d - r)^2. That's a key identity! Indeed:

Let’s compute: (d + R - r)*(R - d + r) = ( (R + (d - r)) ) * ( (R - (d - r)) ) = R^2 - (d - r)^2.

Great. So we have:

R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (d + R - r)*(R - d + r).

Now, also note that (d + R - r) = 2 x_P. And (R - d + r) is positive. So we can simplify the fraction:

\frac{ d r (d + R - r)^2 }{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 } = \frac{ d r (d + R - r)^2 }{ (d + R - r)*(R - d + r) } = \frac{ d r (d + R - r) }{ R - d + r }.

Thus

|P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{ d r (d + R - r) }{ R - d + r } - r^2.

That's much simpler. Good.

Now, also recall that x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So 2 r x_P = r (d + R - r). So we can plug into |v|^2.

Now, let's compute |v|^2 step by step.

|v|^2 = (x_P^2 + y_P^2) + 2 r x_P + r^2.

But we have expression for (x_P^2 + y_P^2) - r^2 = |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{ d r (d + R - r) }{ R - d + r } - r^2.

Thus

|P|^2 = r^2 + \frac{ d r (d + R - r) }{ R - d + r }.

Then

|v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2 = \left( r^2 + \frac{ d r (d + R - r) }{ R - d + r } \right) + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Line AP meets Γ (center N(4,0), radius 5) again at F. Equation: (x-4)^2 + y^2 = 25. Substitute y = -2x+3. Compute (x-4)^2 + (-2x+3)^2 = 25. Expand: (x^2 -8x +16) + (4x^2 -12x +9) = 5x^2 -20x +25 = 25 → 5x^2 -20x = 0 → x(5x-20)=0 → x=0 (A) or x=4. Then y = -2*4+3 = -8+3 = -5. So F = (4, -5). Good.

Thus we have a good example. Now we need to prove generally.

Now we have an analytic proof. Let's proceed with coordinates: M = (0,0), N = (d,0). Let r < R, d > R - r, d < R + r. We have A = (a, h), B = (a, -h) with a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d), h^2 = r^2 - a^2. C = (-r, 0), D = (d+R, 0). P = circumcenter of ACD. We derived y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h with x_P = (d + R - r) / 2.

Now, we also have H = (x_P, y_H) with y_H = \frac{x_P (d - x_P)}{y_P}.

Now, we need to prove that line L: through H parallel to AP (direction v = P - A) is tangent to circle (BEF). Let's compute E and F coordinates using the secant theorem from P. That gave t_E = r (d + r + R) / |v|^2, t_F = R (d + r + R) / |v|^2. That's simpler. But we can also compute E and F as A + t_E (P - A) etc. However note that we derived t_E and t_F using the formula from power, but we need to verify that they are indeed positive and that the product relation yields that. Let's derive carefully.

Given P is a point. The line AP intersects Ω at A and E. So with directed distances from P: let PA = u (signed). Then PE = Power(P, Ω) / u. Then E = P + (Power(P, Ω) / u) * (unit vector in direction of A - P). That's P + (Power(P, Ω) / (|A - P|^2)) * (A - P). So we need Power(P, Ω) = |P|^2 - r^2 (since M is origin). So we can compute that.

Similarly for Γ: Power(P, Γ) = |P - N|^2 - R^2.

Now, if we compute t_E as the factor such that E = A + λ (P - A), we have λ = t_E. Then we found t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2 from the quadratic. That's an expression. But also from power, we have λ = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / |v|^2? Wait earlier we derived λ_E = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / |v|^2. Let's re-derive that to confirm.

We have param X = A + λ (P - A). Then when λ = 1, X = P. Then the directed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) * |P - A|? Actually if we denote v = P - A. Then X = A + λ v = A + λ (P - A). Then vector from P to X is X - P = (A + λ v) - P = (A - P) + λ v = -v + λ v = (λ - 1) v. So signed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) * |v|, with sign depending on direction of v. So the signed distance from P to A is (0 - 1) * |v| = -|v|. But A is on Ω, and P is not. The product of signed distances from P to the two intersection points (A and E) equals Power(P). So if we denote s_A = signed distance PA (from P to A) = -|v| (since A is in direction opposite to v). Then s_E = Power(P) / s_A. Then the parameter λ_E such that X = E is given by (λ_E - 1) v = s_E * (v / |v|) (unit vector). Actually careful: The signed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) |v| times sign of v? Let's define vector w = v = P - A. Then |w| = |v|. Then the unit vector from P towards A is (A - P) / |A - P| = -v / |v|. So the signed distance PA (from P to A) is -|v|. Then the signed distance from P to E is some value t. Then E = P + t * (unit vector in direction of A - P) = P + t * (-v/|v|). But also we have E = A + λ v. So we can relate λ. Let's do it systematically.

Let v = P - A. Then |v| = L. The unit vector from P to A is (A - P) / |A - P| = -v / L. So signed distance PA = -L. Then the other intersection E has signed distance PE = Power(P, Ω) / PA = Power(P) / (-L). So PE = -Power(P) / L. Then E = P + PE * (unit vector in direction of A - P) = P + (-Power(P) / L) * (-v / L) = P + (Power(P) / L^2) * v. So E = P + (Power(P) / |v|^2) * v. But also E = A + λ v. So we have A + λ v = P + (Power(P) / L^2) v. Since P = A + v. Then A + λ v = A + v + (Power(P) / L^2) v => λ v = v + (Power(P) / L^2) v => λ = 1 + Power(P) / L^2. However we earlier derived λ = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / L^2? Let's check: Power(P) = |P|^2 - r^2. So λ = 1 + (|P|^2 - r^2) / L^2. But earlier from quadratic we got t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2. That seems different. Let's compute with numeric example to see which is correct.

In numeric example: M=(0,0), N=(4,0). r=3, R=5. A=(0,3). P=(3,-3). v = P - A = (3,-6). L^2 = 9+36=45. |P|^2 = 9+9=18. |P|^2 - r^2 = 18-9=9. Then λ = 1 + 9/45 = 1 + 0.2 = 1.2. But earlier we found E = A + t_E v with t_E = 2.4? Actually we computed E = (2.4, -1.8) and v = (3,-6). A + t v = (0+3t, 3-6t). Setting t = 0.8 gives (2.4, 3-4.8=-1.8). So t_E = 0.8. That's different from 1.2. So my derivation using power gave λ = 1 + Power(P)/L^2, which gave 1.2, but actual t_E is 0.8. So there is a mistake. Let's examine.

The signed distance from P to A: P = (3,-3), A = (0,3). The vector from P to A is A - P = (-3, 6). Its length is sqrt(9+36)=√45≈6.708. The signed distance PA (from P to A) is the directed distance along the line AP. If we take the direction from P towards A, that's the direction of A - P. The signed distance PA is +|A-P|, because A is in that direction. But earlier I said it's -|v|, but v = P - A is opposite direction. So I need to be careful with signs.

We have line AP. Let's define the line direction. The two points A and P are distinct. The line can be oriented. The signed distance from P to A along the line is something like: we can take the vector from P to A, call it w = A - P. Then the signed distance PA (positive if A is in the direction of w) is |w|. So PA = |A - P|. That's positive. Similarly, the signed distance from P to E, where E is the second intersection, should satisfy PA * PE = Power(P). So PE = Power(P) / PA. Since PA > 0. So PE = (|P|^2 - r^2) / |A - P|.

Now, E lies on the line AP. There are two possibilities: E is on the same side of P as A, or on the opposite side. The signed distance PE could be positive or negative depending on whether E is in the direction of A - P (i.e., same direction as A from P). If PE is positive, then E is on the ray from P towards A; if negative, then it's on the opposite ray. But the product relation with signed distances uses directed distances along the line with a chosen orientation. Usually, for a point P and a line intersecting a circle at X and Y, we have PX * PY = power, where distances are signed with respect to an orientation on the line. If we set an orientation (say from P towards A), then PA is positive, and PE (signed) is the coordinate of E along that orientation. Then PA * PE = power. So PE = power / PA. That's consistent. So we don't need to worry about sign; we just use that expression. But then we need to express E in terms of A and P. Let's denote orientation vector u = A - P (pointing from P to A). Then any point X on line AP can be expressed as P + t * u, where t is a real number. Then t = 0 gives P, t = 1 gives A. So A corresponds to t = 1. Then E corresponds to some t = t_E. Then we have:

PA = |A - P| = |u|. The signed distance from P to A in orientation u is +|u|. The signed distance from P to E is t_E * |u|, with sign depending on t_E. Then PA * PE = |u| * (t_E |u|) = t_E |u|^2. This must equal power. So t_E |u|^2 = power. Therefore t_E = power / |u|^2.

Thus E = P + (power / |u|^2) * u = P + (power / |u|^2) * (A - P). That's a vector expression. That's what we have. So E = P + (power) / |v|^2 * (A - P). But in our earlier notation, we used v = P - A. So A - P = -v. Then E = P + (power) / |v|^2 * (-v) = P - (power / |v|^2) v. That's different from earlier expression where we had E = P + (power / |v|^2) v. So the sign matters. Let's compute with numeric: power = |P|^2 - r^2 = 9. |v|^2 = 45. Then power/|v|^2 = 9/45 = 0.2. Then E = P + 0.2 * (A - P) = P + 0.2 * (-v) = P - 0.2 v. With v = (3,-6). Then P - 0.2 v = (3, -3) - (0.6, -1.2) = (2.4, -1.8). That's correct! So indeed E = P - (power/|v|^2) v. So in our earlier derivation with the quadratic, we had t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2, which gave 0.8, which is not 0.2. That's because we used a different param: A + t (P - A). Let's recalc that param to see relation. We had X(t) = A + t (P - A). Then t = 0 -> A, t = 1 -> P. So in terms of t, the signed distance from A to X is t * |P-A|. The signed distance from P to X is (t-1) * |P-A|. If we want to use power from P, we need signed distances from P. So it's easier to use param with origin at P. That's what we did with t_E. So we should adopt that: let u = A - P. Then E = P + (power_Ω / |u|^2) * u. Similarly, F = P + (power_Γ / |u|^2) * u. So we can compute E and F coordinates without solving quadratic. That's simpler.

Now, we have expressions for power_Ω and power_Γ. Let's compute them in terms of d, r, R.

We have M = (0,0), N = (d,0). P = (x_P, y_P). Then

power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2.
power_Γ = |P - N|^2 - R^2.

We also have |u|^2 = |A - P|^2 = |v|^2. So we need that.

Now, the line L is through H parallel to AP, so direction is u = A - P. So L = H + s u.

We need to prove that L is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to L equals the radius. Equivalently, the power of H with respect to that circle? Not.

Maybe we can find an expression for O and radius that involves u and some known points. Since E and F are expressed as P + α u and P + β u with α = power_Ω / |u|^2, β = power_Γ / |u|^2. So they are collinear with P and A. So the triangle BEF has two vertices on line AP. So its circumcircle can be described. Perhaps we can compute the circle (BEF) by using the fact that A is the radical center of circles (Ω), (Γ), and (BEF)? Not.

Alternatively, we could compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and then show that H lies on the polar of something, but need tangency.

Let's try to compute O and radius explicitly using coordinates, but simplify using algebraic identities. We have:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
r < R
a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d)
h^2 = r^2 - a^2

Define:

x_P = (d + R - r) / 2
y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h

We also have u = A - P. Then compute |u|^2 = |A - P|^2.

We can compute |u|^2 in terms of d, r, R. Let's do that.

We have:

A = (a, h)
P = (x_P, y_P)

So

|u|^2 = (a - x_P)^2 + (h - y_P)^2.

We can expand and simplify using relationships. But earlier we derived an expression for |v|^2 (where v = P - A) using |P|^2 and x_P. That gave:

|v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait we derived: |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That was from |v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2, after substituting a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. Let's confirm that expression.

We had:

|v|^2 = (x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2.
Expand: (x_P^2 - 2 a x_P + a^2) + (y_P^2 - 2 h y_P + h^2) = (x_P^2 + y_P^2) - 2(a x_P + h y_P) + (a^2 + h^2).
Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2. And a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P (derived from perpendicular bisector condition). So

|v|^2 = |P|^2 - 2(- r x_P) + r^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Thus |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's correct.

Now, |P|^2 - r^2 = power_Ω. So |P|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω. Then

|v|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2.

But earlier we had |v|^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait check: |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. Since |P|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω, then |v|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2. That's what we have. That's consistent.

Now, we also have an expression for power_Ω from earlier: power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2 (after simplification). Actually we derived:

|P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2.

But that was based on the expression for |P|^2 - r^2 that simplified. Let's re-derive that cleanly.

We have P = (x_P, y_P). We have y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h. So

|P|^2 = x_P^2 + \frac{x_P^2 (r + a)^2}{h^2} = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{(r + a)^2}{h^2}\right).

Now, (r + a)^2 / h^2 = \frac{(d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} (as derived). So

|P|^2 = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{(d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}\right) = x_P^2 \cdot \frac{R^2 - (d - r)^2 + (d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} = x_P^2 \cdot \frac{4 d r}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Thus

|P|^2 = \frac{4 d r x_P^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Now, x_P^2 = \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{4}. So

|P|^2 = \frac{4 d r}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} \cdot \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{4} = \frac{d r (d + R - r)^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Now, R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - d + r)(R + d - r). And also note that (d + R - r) * (R - d + r) = (R + d - r)(R - d + r) = R^2 - (d - r)^2. So indeed

\frac{(d + R - r)^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} = \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{(d + R - r)(R - d + r)} = \frac{d + R - r}{R - d + r}.

Thus

|P|^2 = d r \cdot \frac{d + R - r}{R - d + r}.

That's neat. So

|P|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r}.

Now, power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2.

Simplify:

power_Ω = \frac{d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r)}{R - d + r}.

Compute numerator:

N1 = d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r) = d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r).

Expand:

d r (d + R - r) = d^2 r + d r R - d r^2.
r^2 (R - d + r) = r^2 R - r^2 d + r^3.

So N1 = d^2 r + d r R - d r^2 - r^2 R + r^2 d - r^3.

Combine like terms: d^2 r, d r R, - d r^2 + r^2 d cancel? Actually - d r^2 + r^2 d = 0. So they cancel. So we get:

N1 = d^2 r + d r R - r^2 R - r^3.

Factor r: N1 = r (d^2 + d R - r R - r^2). That's N1 = r (d^2 + d R - r(R + r)). Not obviously simplifying.

But we might not need to simplify power_Ω further because we will use it in t_E expression. However we also need |u|^2 = |A - P|^2. Let's compute that using the relationship we have: |A - P|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait we derived |v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's |P - A|^2. So |u|^2 = |A - P|^2 = |P - A|^2, same. So |u|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's one expression. Also we can compute |u|^2 directly using coordinates, but maybe this is easier.

Now, we have:

|u|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Plug |P|^2 expression:

|u|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} + 2 r \cdot \frac{d + R - r}{2} + r^2 = \frac{d r T}{V} + r T + 2 r^2, where T = d + R - r, V = R - d + r. That's the same as earlier |v|^2.

Now, we also need α = power_Ω / |u|^2, β = power_Γ / |u|^2. We can compute power_Γ similarly.

Compute |P - N|^2. N = (d,0). Then

|P - N|^2 = (x_P - d)^2 + y_P^2 = (x_P^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2) + y_P^2 = |P|^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2.

Thus

power_Γ = |P - N|^2 - R^2 = |P|^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2 - R^2.

Now, |P|^2 expressed as d r T / V. So

power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - 2 d x_P + d^2 - R^2.

Now, x_P = T/2. So 2 d x_P = d T.

Thus power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - d T + d^2 - R^2.

Now, we can also find a relation between d^2 - R^2 and something like -2 d a? Actually from a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d), we have d^2 - R^2 = 2 d a - r^2. So d^2 - R^2 = 2 d a - r^2.

Thus

power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - d T + (2 d a - r^2).

But maybe it's simpler to compute power_Γ using the expression for |P|^2 and also using the fact that A is on Γ. Another approach: Since A and N are known, we have |A - N|^2 = R^2. |O - E|^2 = |v|^2 ( (μ-λ)^2/4 + s^2 ).   (1)

Thus

| (0, 2h) + ((λ+μ)/2) v + s w |^2 = |v|^2 ( (μ-λ)^2/4 + s^2 ).   (2)

Let’s denote X = (0, 2h) + ((λ+μ)/2) v. Then O - B = X + s w. Since v and w are orthogonal, and also (0,2h) may have components along v and w. |X|^2 + s^2 |v|^2 + 2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 (s^2 + ((μ-λ)^2/4)). 

Cancel s^2 |v|^2 on both sides? Left has s^2 |v|^2, right has |v|^2 s^2, they cancel. So we get:

|X|^2 + 2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4).

Thus

2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4) - |X|^2.

So s = [ |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4) - |X|^2 ] / (2 (X·w)), provided X·w ≠ 0. That's a linear equation for s. So we can compute s. That seems manageable. Then we can compute O. We could compute s and then simplify.

But we also have h^2 = r^2 - a^2. Let's compute a^2 explicitly: a^2 = \frac{(1 + r^2 - R^2)^2}{4}. So

2r - (1 + r^2 - R^2) = 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2 = R^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1. That's not obviously nice. But we can also use the formula we derived earlier: h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{4}. That's a common expression. Let's check: With d=1, we have:

h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{4}. That's from earlier formula with d=1: h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2} = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{4}. Yes.

So h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{4}. That's a nice product. Now, x_P = (1 + R - r)/2. Also, r + a = ((r+1)^2 - R^2)/2. That's similar to ((1 + r)^2 - R^2)/2. So indeed, r + a = \frac{ ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{2}. That's interesting. So we have:

r + a = \frac{ ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{2}.

And h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) }{4} = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) * 2 (r + a) }{4}? Since ((1 + r)^2 - R^2) = 2 (r + a). So h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) * 2 (r + a) }{4} = \frac{ (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) (r + a) }{2}.

Thus h^2 = \frac{ (r + a) (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) }{2}.

Now, N = x_P (r + a) + h^2 = \frac{1 + R - r}{2} (r + a) + \frac{ (r + a) (R^2 - (1 - r)^2) }{2} = \frac{ (r + a) }{2} \left( 1 + R - r + R^2 - (1 - r)^2 \right).

Simplify the bracket:

1 + R - r + R^2 - (1 - r)^2 = 1 + R - r + R^2 - (1 - 2r + r^2) = 1 + R - r + R^2 - 1 + 2r - r^2 = (R - r + 2r) + R^2 - r^2 = R + r + R^2 - r^2.

Thus N = \frac{ (r + a) }{2} \left( R + r + R^2 - r^2 \right). But note that R^2 - r^2 = (R - r)(R + r). So

N = \frac{ (r + a) }{2} \left( R + r + (R - r)(R + r) \right). Not sure if that simplifies.

But we also have expression for dy = - N / h. So dy = - \frac{ (r + a) }{2 h} ( R + r + R^2 - r^2 ). That's messy.

Let's try a different coordinate approach: Place M = (0,0), N = (1,0). Then we have A = (a, h). We also have P = (x_P, y_P). We can compute everything. Then H = (x_P, y_H). Let's denote:

Let A and P be known. Then v = P - A. Then

E = A + λ v
F = A + μ v

with λ, μ as above.

Now, B is the reflection of A across MN, which is horizontal. So B = A - (0, 2h) where (0,2h) is vertical. But note that h is not independent; it's related to A and M,N. Now, we can compute the equations for O. Let's write O = (X,Y). Then:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (X - a)^2 + (Y + h)^2 = (X - (a + λ dx))^2 + (Y - (h + λ dy))^2. 

Similarly, |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → (X - a)^2 + (Y + h)^2 = (X - (a + μ dx))^2 + (Y - (h + μ dy))^2.

Let's simplify the difference between the two equations to get O·v. Indeed, subtract the two equations:

|O - E|^2 - |O - F|^2 = 0 → (|O - E|^2 - |O - F|^2) = 0 → (O - E)·(O - E) - (O - F)·(O - F) = 0 → -2 O·E + |E|^2 + 2 O·F - |F|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2. Since F - E = (μ - λ) v. So

2 (μ - λ) O·v = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Thus O·v = (|F|^2 - |E|^2) / (2 (μ - λ)). That's one equation. So we can compute O·v.

Now, the other equation from B and E gives: 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. But we can also use B and F. They are equivalent given the O·v equation. So we have two independent linear equations: one involving O·v, another involving O·(E - B). That's enough to solve for O. However O is a vector; O·v and O·(E - B) are scalars. But we need O itself. Since we have two linear equations, but O has two components. In 2D, two independent linear equations determine O uniquely (if they are independent). However O·v is a linear equation in O, and O·(E - B) is another linear equation. So indeed, we can solve for O. That's a system of two linear equations. That's easier than solving with M_EF.

Let's try that. We'll need to compute E - B and |E|^2 - |B|^2. Then we can solve for O.

So we can treat O as (X,Y). Then we have:

Simplify these to linear equations. Let's do that.

Take the difference between (1) and (2) (they both equal |O-B|^2). Actually we have two equations that both assert that |O-B|^2 equals |O-E|^2 and |O-F|^2. So we can set |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2, which gives a linear equation. That's what we did: O·v = (|F|^2 - |E|^2) / (2 (μ - λ)). So that's one linear equation.

Then we also have |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, which gives another linear equation.

So we can solve these two linear equations.

Let's compute |O-E|^2 - |O-F|^2 = 0 → (O-E)·(O-E) - (O-F)·(O-F) = 0 → -2 O·E + |E|^2 + 2 O·F - |F|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Since F - E = (μ - λ) v. So 2 (μ - λ) O·v = |F|^2 - |E|^2 → O·v = \frac{ |F|^2 - |E|^2 }{2 (μ - λ)}. (EqA)

Now, compute |O-B|^2 - |O-E|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. (EqB)

Thus we have two linear equations.

Given that we have expressions for A·v and |v|^2, maybe we can compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 and |F|^2 - |E|^2 nicely. Let's attempt.

|B|^2 = |(a, -h)|^2 = a^2 + h^2 = r^2.

|F|^2 = |A|^2 + 2μ A·v + μ^2 |v|^2.

|v|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Thus

So dy = - \frac{N}{h} with N = x_P (r + a) + h^2.

Now, note that h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So

N = x_P (r + a) + r^2 - a^2 = x_P (r + a) + (r - a)(r + a) = (r + a)(x_P + r - a).

Thus N = (r + a)(x_P + r - a). That's a factorization. That's nice! Because we can combine.

Now, x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So

N = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R - r}{2} + r - a ) = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R - r + 2r - 2a}{2} ) = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R + r - 2a}{2} ).

But we also have a expression for a in terms of d, r, R. Maybe we can simplify further.

Compute d + R + r - 2a. Since a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So 2a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / d. Then

d + R + r - 2a = d + R + r - \frac{ d^2 + r^2 - R^2 }{ d } = \frac{ d^2 + dR + dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) }{ d } = \frac{ dR + dr - r^2 + R^2 }{ d }.

So

N = (r + a) * \frac{ dR + dr - r^2 + R^2 }{ 2d }.

But that seems messy. However we also have an expression for r + a. Let's compute r + a in terms of d, r, R. We already have r + a = \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{ 2d }.

But earlier we derived N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ 4 d^2 }? That was after simplifying with the product we had. Let's check: We had N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ 4 d^2 }. That's a different expression. Let's reconcile.

We have N = x_P (r + a) + h^2. And we expressed h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2}. And x_P (r + a) = \frac{ (d + R - r) }{2} * \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{2 d } = \frac{ (d + R - r) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d}. So

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d} ( d + R - r ) + \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 }.

Factor \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 } times something. Let's combine:

First term: \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) d }{4 d^2}? Actually multiply numerator and denominator by d to get denominator 4 d^2: \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) d }{4 d^2}. That's not simpler. Let's compute common denominator 4 d^2.

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} + \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 } = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4} \left( \frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } \right).

Now we already simplified the bracket to \frac{ (R + r)(d + R - r) }{ d^2 }? Let's redo that simplification:

Let’s compute \frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } = \frac{ d (d + R - r) + (R^2 - (d - r)^2) }{ d^2 }.

Now compute numerator: d(d + R - r) = d^2 + dR - dr. And R^2 - (d - r)^2 = R^2 - (d^2 - 2dr + r^2) = R^2 - d^2 + 2dr - r^2. Sum: (d^2 + dR - dr) + (R^2 - d^2 + 2dr - r^2) = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2. So numerator = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2.

Now, note that (d + R - r)(R + r) = (d + R - r)(R + r) = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2. Indeed, compute: (d + R - r)*(R + r) = d(R + r) + (R - r)*(R + r) = dR + dr + (R^2 - r^2). That's exactly the numerator. So numerator = (d + R - r)(R + r). Thus

\frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } = \frac{ (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ d^2 }.

Therefore

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4} * \frac{ (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ d^2 }.

So N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{4 d^2 }.

That's what we had. Good. So N involves ((d + r)^2 - R^2) = (d + r - R)(d + r + R). That's a product of three factors. So dy = - N / h.

Now, we also have |v|^2 = dx^2 + dy^2. So we could compute |v|^2 using that expression. But maybe we can also compute |v|^2 from the identity:

x_P = (d + R - r)/2.

We have C = (-r, 0). So distance squared from P to C: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Distance squared from P to D: (x_P - (d+R))^2 + y_P^2.

Since P is on perpendicular bisector of CD, these are equal automatically? Actually if P is on the perpendicular bisector, then distances to C and D are equal. So that's satisfied by x_P. So we don't need to enforce equality, it's already satisfied by that x-coordinate. Then we need P to be equidistant to A and C (or A and D). So set |P A|^2 = |P C|^2.

Compute |P C|^2: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2. But note that x_P + r = (d + R - r)/2 + r = (d + R - r + 2r)/2 = (d + R + r)/2. So that's (d + R + r)/2.

Similarly, |P D|^2: (x_P - (d+R))^2 + y_P^2. x_P - (d+R) = (d + R - r)/2 - (d+R) = (d + R - r - 2d - 2R)/2 = (-d - R - r)/2 = -(d + R + r)/2. Squared gives same. So indeed equal.

Now compute |P A|^2: (x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2. Set equal to (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Thus:

(x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:

(x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2) + (y_P^2 - 2h y_P + h^2) = (x_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2) + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2:

-2a x_P + a^2 - 2h y_P + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Bring terms:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2 (since A is on Ω). So a^2 + h^2 = r^2. Then:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel r^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P = 2r x_P.

Divide 2:

- a x_P - h y_P = r x_P.

Thus:

- h y_P = r x_P + a x_P = x_P (r + a).

So:

y_P = - (x_P (r + a)) / h.

That's a much simpler expression! Let's double-check the algebra.

We had:

(x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

Compute:

(x_P - a)^2 = x_P^2 - 2a x_P + a^2.
(y_P - h)^2 = y_P^2 - 2h y_P + h^2.
Sum = x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2.

Right side: (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2 = x_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2 + y_P^2.

Set equal:

x_P^2 + y_P^2 - 2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2 + 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel x_P^2 + y_P^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + a^2 + h^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2. So:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P + r^2 = 2r x_P + r^2.

Cancel r^2:

-2a x_P - 2h y_P = 2r x_P.

Divide by 2:

- a x_P - h y_P = r x_P.

Thus:

- h y_P = r x_P + a x_P = x_P (r + a).

So y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h.

Then r + a = r + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = (2dr + d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d).

That's interesting. Similarly, we might compute h^2 = r^2 - a^2.

Now, h appears in denominator. We can also express h^2 in terms of d, r, R.

Compute a^2:

a^2 = ( (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) / (4d^2).

Then h^2 = r^2 - a^2 = (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) / (4d^2). That's a common expression (related to the area of triangle formed by centers and intersection). There is formula for common chord length: AB = (1/d) * sqrt( ( (R + r)^2 - d^2) * (d^2 - (R - r)^2) ) ). That's the length of the common chord. Then half-length h = AB/2. But we can compute h^2 directly. Let's derive:

We have h^2 = r^2 - a^2, where a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So:

4d^2 h^2 = 4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2.

That's a factorization: (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2) = ( (2dr)^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 ) = (2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)) * (2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)). Let's compute:

2dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = -d^2 - r^2 + 2dr + R^2 = R^2 - (d^2 - 2dr + r^2) = R^2 - (d - r)^2.

Similarly, 2dr + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2 = (d + r)^2 - R^2.

Thus:

4d^2 h^2 = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) * ((d + r)^2 - R^2).

Since circles intersect, both factors are positive: R^2 - (d - r)^2 > 0 (because d > |R - r|) and (d + r)^2 - R^2 > 0 (because d < R + r). So indeed positive.

Thus h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4d^2}.

We also have (r + a) expression: (r + a) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d). So indeed (r + a) = ((d + r)^2 - R^2) / (2d). That's positive.

Thus PA * PE = |A - P| * ( (1 - λ_E) |A - P| ) = (1 - λ_E) |A - P|^2. But the power relation gives PA * PE = Power(P,Ω). So (1 - λ_E) |A - P|^2 = Power(P,Ω). Therefore λ_E = 1 - Power(P,Ω) / |A - P|^2. Similarly, λ_F = 1 - Power(P,Γ) / |A - P|^2.

Thus we have a good example. Now we need to prove generally.

Now we have an analytic proof. Let's proceed with coordinates: M = (0,0), N = (d,0). Let r < R, d > R - r, d < R + r. We have A = (a, h), B = (a, -h) with a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d), h^2 = r^2 - a^2. C = (-r, 0), D = (d+R, 0). P = circumcenter of ACD. We derived y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h with x_P = (d + R - r) / 2.

Now, we also have H = (x_P, y_H) with y_H = \frac{x_P (d - x_P)}{y_P}.

Now, we need to prove that line L: through H parallel to AP (direction v = P - A) is tangent to circle (BEF). Let's compute E and F coordinates using the secant theorem from P. That gave t_E = r (d + r + R) / |v|^2, t_F = R (d + r + R) / |v|^2. That's simpler. But we can also compute E and F as A + t_E (P - A) etc. However note that we derived t_E and t_F using the formula from power, but we need to verify that they are indeed positive and that the product relation yields that. Let's derive carefully.

Given P is a point. The line AP intersects Ω at A and E. So with directed distances from P: let PA = u (signed). Then PE = Power(P, Ω) / u. Then E = P + (Power(P, Ω) / u) * (unit vector in direction of A - P). That's P + (Power(P, Ω) / (|A - P|^2)) * (A - P). So we need Power(P, Ω) = |P|^2 - r^2 (since M is origin). So we can compute that.

Similarly for Γ: Power(P, Γ) = |P - N|^2 - R^2.

Now, if we compute t_E as the factor such that E = A + λ (P - A), we have λ = t_E. Then we found t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2 from the quadratic. That's an expression. But also from power, we have λ = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / |v|^2? Wait earlier we derived λ_E = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / |v|^2. Let's re-derive that to confirm.

We have param X = A + λ (P - A). Then when λ = 1, X = P. Then the directed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) * |P - A|? Actually if we denote v = P - A. Then X = A + λ v = A + λ (P - A). Then vector from P to X is X - P = (A + λ v) - P = (A - P) + λ v = -v + λ v = (λ - 1) v. So signed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) * |v|, with sign depending on direction of v. So the signed distance from P to A is (0 - 1) * |v| = -|v|. But A is on Ω, and P is not. The product of signed distances from P to the two intersection points (A and E) equals Power(P). So if we denote s_A = signed distance PA (from P to A) = -|v| (since A is in direction opposite to v). Then s_E = Power(P) / s_A. Then the parameter λ_E such that X = E is given by (λ_E - 1) v = s_E * (v / |v|) (unit vector). Actually careful: The signed distance from P to X is (λ - 1) |v| times sign of v? Let's define vector w = v = P - A. Then |w| = |v|. Then the unit vector from P towards A is (A - P) / |A - P| = -v / |v|. So the signed distance PA (from P to A) is -|v|. Then the signed distance from P to E is some value t. Then E = P + t * (unit vector in direction of A - P) = P + t * (-v/|v|). But also we have E = A + λ v. So we can relate λ. Let's do it systematically.

Let v = P - A. Then |v| = L. The unit vector from P to A is (A - P) / |A - P| = -v / L. So signed distance PA = -L. Then the other intersection E has signed distance PE = Power(P, Ω) / PA = Power(P) / (-L). So PE = -Power(P) / L. Then E = P + PE * (unit vector in direction of A - P) = P + (-Power(P) / L) * (-v / L) = P + (Power(P) / L^2) * v. So E = P + (Power(P) / |v|^2) * v. But also E = A + λ v. So we have A + λ v = P + (Power(P) / L^2) v. Since P = A + v. Then A + λ v = A + v + (Power(P) / L^2) v => λ v = v + (Power(P) / L^2) v => λ = 1 + Power(P) / L^2. However we earlier derived λ = 1 - (|P|^2 - r^2) / L^2? Let's check: Power(P) = |P|^2 - r^2. So λ = 1 + (|P|^2 - r^2) / L^2. But earlier from quadratic we got t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2. That seems different. Let's compute with numeric example to see which is correct.

In numeric example: M=(0,0), N=(4,0). r=3, R=5. A=(0,3). P=(3,-3). v = P - A = (3,-6). L^2 = 9+36=45. |P|^2 = 9+9=18. |P|^2 - r^2 = 18-9=9. Then λ = 1 + 9/45 = 1 + 0.2 = 1.2. But earlier we found E = A + t_E v with t_E = 2.4? Actually we computed E = (2.4, -1.8) and v = (3,-6). A + t v = (0+3t, 3-6t). Setting t = 0.8 gives (2.4, 3-4.8=-1.8). So t_E = 0.8. That's different from 1.2. So my derivation using power gave λ = 1 + Power(P)/L^2, which gave 1.2, but actual t_E is 0.8. So there is a mistake. Let's examine.

The signed distance from P to A: P = (3,-3), A = (0,3). The vector from P to A is A - P = (-3, 6). Its length is sqrt(9+36)=√45≈6.708. The signed distance PA (from P to A) is the directed distance along the line AP. If we take the direction from P towards A, that's the direction of A - P. The signed distance PA is +|A-P|, because A is in that direction. But earlier I said it's -|v|, but v = P - A is opposite direction. So I need to be careful with signs.

We have line AP. Let's define the line direction. The two points A and P are distinct. The line can be oriented. The signed distance from P to A along the line is something like: we can take the vector from P to A, call it w = A - P. Then the signed distance PA (positive if A is in the direction of w) is |w|. So PA = |A - P|. That's positive. Similarly, the signed distance from P to E, where E is the second intersection, should satisfy PA * PE = Power(P). So PE = Power(P) / PA. Since PA > 0. So PE = (|P|^2 - r^2) / |A - P|.

Now, E lies on the line AP. There are two possibilities: E is on the same side of P as A, or on the opposite side. The signed distance PE could be positive or negative depending on whether E is in the direction of A - P (i.e., same direction as A from P). If PE is positive, then E is on the ray from P towards A; if negative, then it's on the opposite ray. But the product relation with signed distances uses directed distances along the line with a chosen orientation. Usually, for a point P and a line intersecting a circle at X and Y, we have PX * PY = power, where distances are signed with respect to an orientation on the line. If we set an orientation (say from P towards A), then PA is positive, and PE (signed) is the coordinate of E along that orientation. Then PA * PE = power. So PE = power / PA. That's consistent. So we don't need to worry about sign; we just use that expression. But then we need to express E in terms of A and P. Let's denote orientation vector u = A - P (pointing from P to A). Then any point X on line AP can be expressed as P + t * u, where t is a real number. Then t = 0 gives P, t = 1 gives A. So A corresponds to t = 1. Then E corresponds to some t = t_E. Then we have:

PA = |A - P| = |u|. The signed distance from P to A in orientation u is +|u|. The signed distance from P to E is t_E * |u|, with sign depending on t_E. Then PA * PE = |u| * (t_E |u|) = t_E |u|^2. This must equal power. So t_E |u|^2 = power. Therefore t_E = power / |u|^2.

Thus E = P + (power / |u|^2) * u = P + (power / |u|^2) * (A - P). That's a vector expression. That's what we have. So E = P + (power) / |v|^2 * (A - P). But in our earlier notation, we used v = P - A. So A - P = -v. Then E = P + (power) / |v|^2 * (-v) = P - (power / |v|^2) v. That's different from earlier expression where we had E = P + (power / |v|^2) v. So the sign matters. Let's compute with numeric: power = |P|^2 - r^2 = 9. |v|^2 = 45. Then power/|v|^2 = 9/45 = 0.2. Then E = P + 0.2 * (A - P) = P + 0.2 * (-v) = P - 0.2 v. With v = (3,-6). Then P - 0.2 v = (3, -3) - (0.6, -1.2) = (2.4, -1.8). That's correct! So indeed E = P - (power/|v|^2) v. So in our earlier derivation with the quadratic, we had t_E = 2 r (x_P + r) / |v|^2, which gave 0.8, which is not 0.2. That's because we used a different param: A + t (P - A). Let's recalc that param to see relation. We had X(t) = A + t (P - A). Then t = 0 -> A, t = 1 -> P. So in terms of t, the signed distance from A to X is t * |P-A|. The signed distance from P to X is (t-1) * |P-A|. If we want to use power from P, we need signed distances from P. So it's easier to use param with origin at P. That's what we did with t_E. So we should adopt that: let u = A - P. Then E = P + (power_Ω / |u|^2) * u. Similarly, F = P + (power_Γ / |u|^2) * u. So we can compute E and F coordinates without solving quadratic. That's simpler.

Now, we have expressions for power_Ω and power_Γ. Let's compute them in terms of d, r, R.

We have M = (0,0), N = (d,0). P = (x_P, y_P). Then

power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2.
power_Γ = |P - N|^2 - R^2.

We also have |u|^2 = |A - P|^2 = |v|^2. So we need that.

Now, the line L is through H parallel to AP, so direction is u = A - P. So L = H + s u.

We need to prove that L is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the distance from the circumcenter O of triangle BEF to L equals the radius. Equivalently, the power of H with respect to that circle? Not.

Maybe we can find an expression for O and radius that involves u and some known points. Since E and F are expressed as P + α u and P + β u with α = power_Ω / |u|^2, β = power_Γ / |u|^2. So they are collinear with P and A. So the triangle BEF has two vertices on line AP. So its circumcircle can be described. Perhaps we can compute the circle (BEF) by using the fact that A is the radical center of circles (Ω), (Γ), and (BEF)? Not.

Alternatively, we could compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and then show that H lies on the polar of something, but need tangency.

Let's try to compute O and radius explicitly using coordinates, but simplify using algebraic identities. We have:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
r < R
a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d)
h^2 = r^2 - a^2

Define:

x_P = (d + R - r) / 2
y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h

We also have u = A - P. Then compute |u|^2 = |A - P|^2.

We can compute |u|^2 in terms of d, r, R. Let's do that.

We have:

A = (a, h)
P = (x_P, y_P)

So

|u|^2 = (a - x_P)^2 + (h - y_P)^2.

We can expand and simplify using relationships. But earlier we derived an expression for |v|^2 (where v = P - A) using |P|^2 and x_P. That gave:

|v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait we derived: |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That was from |v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2, after substituting a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. Let's confirm that expression.

We had:

|v|^2 = (x_P - a)^2 + (y_P - h)^2.
Expand: (x_P^2 - 2 a x_P + a^2) + (y_P^2 - 2 h y_P + h^2) = (x_P^2 + y_P^2) - 2(a x_P + h y_P) + (a^2 + h^2).
Now a^2 + h^2 = r^2. And a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P (derived from perpendicular bisector condition). So

|v|^2 = |P|^2 - 2(- r x_P) + r^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Thus |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's correct.

Now, |P|^2 - r^2 = power_Ω. So |P|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω. Then

|v|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2.

But earlier we had |v|^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait check: |v|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. Since |P|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω, then |v|^2 = r^2 + power_Ω + 2 r x_P + r^2 = power_Ω + 2 r x_P + 2 r^2. That's what we have. That's consistent.

Now, we also have an expression for power_Ω from earlier: power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2 (after simplification). Actually we derived:

|P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2.

But that was based on the expression for |P|^2 - r^2 that simplified. Let's re-derive that cleanly.

We have P = (x_P, y_P). We have y_P = - x_P (r + a) / h. So

|P|^2 = x_P^2 + \frac{x_P^2 (r + a)^2}{h^2} = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{(r + a)^2}{h^2}\right).

Now, (r + a)^2 / h^2 = \frac{(d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} (as derived). So

|P|^2 = x_P^2 \left(1 + \frac{(d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}\right) = x_P^2 \cdot \frac{R^2 - (d - r)^2 + (d + r)^2 - R^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} = x_P^2 \cdot \frac{4 d r}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Thus

|P|^2 = \frac{4 d r x_P^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Now, x_P^2 = \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{4}. So

|P|^2 = \frac{4 d r}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} \cdot \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{4} = \frac{d r (d + R - r)^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2}.

Now, R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - d + r)(R + d - r). And also note that (d + R - r) * (R - d + r) = (R + d - r)(R - d + r) = R^2 - (d - r)^2. So indeed

\frac{(d + R - r)^2}{R^2 - (d - r)^2} = \frac{(d + R - r)^2}{(d + R - r)(R - d + r)} = \frac{d + R - r}{R - d + r}.

Thus

|P|^2 = d r \cdot \frac{d + R - r}{R - d + r}.

That's neat. So

|P|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r}.

Now, power_Ω = |P|^2 - r^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} - r^2.

Simplify:

power_Ω = \frac{d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r)}{R - d + r}.

Compute numerator:

N1 = d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r) = d r (d + R - r) - r^2 (R - d + r).

Expand:

d r (d + R - r) = d^2 r + d r R - d r^2.
r^2 (R - d + r) = r^2 R - r^2 d + r^3.

So N1 = d^2 r + d r R - d r^2 - r^2 R + r^2 d - r^3.

Combine like terms: d^2 r, d r R, - d r^2 + r^2 d cancel? Actually - d r^2 + r^2 d = 0. So they cancel. So we get:

N1 = d^2 r + d r R - r^2 R - r^3.

Factor r: N1 = r (d^2 + d R - r R - r^2). That's N1 = r (d^2 + d R - r(R + r)). Not obviously simplifying.

But we might not need to simplify power_Ω further because we will use it in t_E expression. However we also need |u|^2 = |A - P|^2. Let's compute that using the relationship we have: |A - P|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2? Wait we derived |v|^2 = |P - A|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's |P - A|^2. So |u|^2 = |A - P|^2 = |P - A|^2, same. So |u|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2. That's one expression. Also we can compute |u|^2 directly using coordinates, but maybe this is easier.

Now, we have:

|u|^2 = |P|^2 + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Plug |P|^2 expression:

|u|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} + 2 r \cdot \frac{d + R - r}{2} + r^2 = \frac{d r T}{V} + r T + 2 r^2, where T = d + R - r, V = R - d + r. That's the same as earlier |v|^2.

Now, we also need α = power_Ω / |u|^2, β = power_Γ / |u|^2. We can compute power_Γ similarly.

Compute |P - N|^2. N = (d,0). Then

|P - N|^2 = (x_P - d)^2 + y_P^2 = (x_P^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2) + y_P^2 = |P|^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2.

Thus

power_Γ = |P - N|^2 - R^2 = |P|^2 - 2 d x_P + d^2 - R^2.

Now, |P|^2 expressed as d r T / V. So

power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - 2 d x_P + d^2 - R^2.

Now, x_P = T/2. So 2 d x_P = d T.

Thus power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - d T + d^2 - R^2.

Now, we can also find a relation between d^2 - R^2 and something like -2 d a? Actually from a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d), we have d^2 - R^2 = 2 d a - r^2. So d^2 - R^2 = 2 d a - r^2.

Thus

power_Γ = \frac{d r T}{V} - d T + (2 d a - r^2).

But maybe it's simpler to compute power_Γ using the expression for |P|^2 and also using the fact that A is on Γ. Another approach: Since A and N are known, we have |A - N|^2 = R^2. Then |P - N|^2 - R^2 = (|P - N|^2 - |A - N|^2) = (P - N)·(P - N) - (A - N)·(A - N) = (P·P - 2 P·N + N·N) - (A·A - 2 A·N + N·N) = |P|^2 - |A|^2 - 2 N·(P - A). Since |A|^2 = r^2. So

power_Γ = (|P|^2 - r^2) - 2 N·(P - A). But N·(P - A) = (d,0)·(P - A) = d (x_P - a). So

power_Γ = (|P|^2 - r^2) - 2 d (x_P - a).

Now, |P|^2 - r^2 = power_Ω. So

power_Γ = power_Ω - 2 d (x_P - a).

Now, x_P - a = \frac{ (R - r) (d + R + r) }{2d} > 0. So we can compute that.

This might be simpler than expanding.

But maybe we don't need power_Γ explicitly. We can compute t_F from the quadratic as earlier, which gave t_F = \frac{R (d + r + R)}{|u|^2}. That expression came from solving (A - N)·v = -R(r+d+R)/2? Wait we derived t_F = \frac{R(r + d + R)}{|v|^2} from the condition that (A - N)·v = -R(r+d+R)/2? Let's revisit that derivation. We had param X = A + t v, with v = P - A. Then we derived t_E = \frac{2 r (x_P + r)}{|v|^2} and t_F = \frac{R(r + d + R)}{|v|^2} after some simplifications. That seems plausible. But we should double-check the derivation with the quadratic method, because that gave a direct expression, not relying on power. However we must ensure that the expression for t_F is correct. Let's recompute using the quadratic for Γ.

We have A on Γ: |A - N|^2 = R^2. v = P - A. Then param: X = A + t v. Then

|X - N|^2 = |(A - N) + t v|^2 = |A - N|^2 + 2 t (A - N)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 t (A - N)·v + t^2 |v|^2.

Set equal to R^2 → 2 t (A - N)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 (A - N)·v + t |v|^2) = 0. So t = 0 or t = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2. So t_F = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2.

Now, we earlier computed (A - N)·v = (a - d)(x_P - a) + h (y_P - h). That's messy. But we derived that (A - N)·v = - \frac{R(r + d + R)}{2}. Wait we derived (A - N)·v = -R(r+d+R)/2? Actually we got (A - N)·v = -R(r + d + R)/2? Let's check the earlier derivation. We had:

(A - N)·v = (a - d)(x_P - a) + h(y_P - h). Then we computed a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. Then we expressed (a - d) x_P + h y_P = a x_P - d x_P + h y_P = (- r x_P) - d x_P = -(r + d) x_P. So (A - N)·v = (a - d)(x_P - a) + h(y_P - h). Expand: (a - d)x_P - (a - d)a + h y_P - h^2. That's (a - d)x_P + h y_P - a(a - d) - h^2? Actually careful: (a - d)(x_P - a) = (a - d)x_P - (a - d)a. Then add h(y_P - h) = h y_P - h^2. So sum = (a - d)x_P + h y_P - a(a - d) - h^2.

But we have a(a - d) = a^2 - a d. So

(A - N)·v = (a - d)x_P + h y_P - a^2 + a d - h^2.

Now, a^2 + h^2 = r^2 → - a^2 - h^2 = -r^2. So - a^2 - h^2 = -r^2. But we have - a^2 - h^2 + a d? Actually it's - a^2 + a d - h^2 = -(a^2 + h^2) + a d = -r^2 + a d.

Thus (A - N)·v = (a - d)x_P + h y_P - r^2 + a d.

Now, we also have a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. So (a - d)x_P + h y_P = a x_P - d x_P + h y_P = - r x_P - d x_P = -(r + d) x_P.

Thus (A - N)·v = -(r + d) x_P - r^2 + a d.

Now, a d = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/2. So

(A - N)·v = -(r + d) x_P - r^2 + \frac{d^2 + r^2 - R^2}{2}.

Now, x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So -(r + d) x_P = -(r + d) * (d + R - r)/2 = -\frac{(r + d)(d + R - r)}{2}. Simplify numerator: (r + d)(d + R - r) = (d + r)(d + R - r). Expand: (d + r)(d + R - r) = d^2 + dR - d r + d r + R r - r^2? Actually compute: (d + r)*(d + R - r) = d*(d + R - r) + r*(d + R - r) = d^2 + dR - d r + r d + r R - r^2 = d^2 + dR + r R - r^2. So that's d^2 + dR + rR - r^2. So

-(r + d) x_P = -\frac{d^2 + dR + rR - r^2}{2}.

Now, combine with - r^2 + \frac{d^2 + r^2 - R^2}{2}. Let's compute the sum:

S = -\frac{d^2 + dR + rR - r^2}{2} - r^2 + \frac{d^2 + r^2 - R^2}{2}.

Combine the fractions over denominator 2:

S = \frac{ - (d^2 + dR + rR - r^2) + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) }{2} - r^2.

Simplify numerator:

- (d^2 + dR + rR - r^2) + (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) = -d^2 - dR - rR + r^2 + d^2 + r^2 - R^2 = (-d^2 + d^2) + (-dR) + (-rR) + (r^2 + r^2) - R^2 = -dR - rR + 2 r^2 - R^2.

So

S = \frac{ -dR - rR + 2 r^2 - R^2 }{2} - r^2.

Now, - r^2 = - (2 r^2)/2, so combine with fraction:

S = \frac{ -dR - rR + 2 r^2 - R^2 - 2 r^2 }{2} = \frac{ -dR - rR - R^2 }{2} = -\frac{ R (d + r + R) }{2}.

Thus (A - N)·v = -\frac{ R (d + r + R) }{2}. Great.

Let's try a more systematic analytic approach with coordinates. Since we have derived expressions for λ and μ in terms of |v|^2, and we have dx and dy, we can compute the coordinates of E and F. Then we can compute the circumcenter O of BEF. That involves solving two equations. But perhaps we can use the fact that O is the point such that the vector from O to the midpoint of EF is perpendicular to v, and also O lies on perpendicular bisector of BE. So we can find O by solving a linear equation (perpendicular to v) and another linear equation (perpendicular bisector). That's a system of two linear equations in the coordinates of O. Since we have an expression for s, we can solve for s. That seems promising.

Let's do that.

Let’s denote:

v = (dx, dy). w = (-dy, dx) (a perpendicular vector). Then any vector perpendicular to v is scalar multiple of w.

We have O = M_EF + s w, where M_EF = A + ((λ+μ)/2) v.

Now, the condition |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 yields an equation that we can solve for s. Since the distances involve squares, it's quadratic in s. But maybe we can simplify to a linear equation because the quadratic terms cancel due to orthogonality? Let's compute.

|O - E|^2 = |((μ-λ)/2) v + s w|^2 = ((μ-λ)^2/4) |v|^2 + s^2 |v|^2. So it's a quadratic in s: |v|^2 (s^2 + ((μ-λ)^2/4)). 

|O - B|^2 = | (0, 2h) + ((λ+μ)/2) v + s w |^2. Expand:

Let’s denote X = (0, 2h) + ((λ+μ)/2) v. Then O - B = X + s w. Since v and w are orthogonal, and also (0,2h) may have components along v and w. But we can compute |X + s w|^2 = |X|^2 + s^2 |w|^2 + 2 s (X·w). Because w is orthogonal to v, but X may have a component along w, and also along v. So we need to compute X·w. 

Now, we set |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. This gives:

|X|^2 + s^2 |v|^2 + 2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 (s^2 + ((μ-λ)^2/4)). 

Cancel s^2 |v|^2 on both sides? Left has s^2 |v|^2, right has |v|^2 s^2, they cancel. So we get:

|X|^2 + 2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4).

Thus

2 s (X·w) = |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4) - |X|^2.

So s = [ |v|^2 ((μ-λ)^2/4) - |X|^2 ] / (2 (X·w)), provided X·w ≠ 0. That's a linear equation for s. So we can compute s. That seems manageable. Then we can compute O. Then compute distance from O to L. That distance is |(H - O) projected onto normal vector of L| divided by |normal|. Since L is parallel to v, a normal vector is w (or any vector perpendicular to v). So the distance from O to line L is |(H - O) · w| / |w|. Because w is perpendicular to v, and L is line through H with direction v. So the distance is |(H - O) · w| / |w|.

Thus we need to prove that |(H - O) · w| / |w| = radius R_circ. That's equivalent to |(H - O) · w| = |w| * R_circ = |v| * R_circ.

Now, O is expressed as M_EF + s w. So H - O = H - M_EF - s w. Then (H - O)·w = (H - M_EF)·w - s |w|^2. But |w|^2 = |v|^2. So

(H - O)·w = (H - M_EF)·w - s |v|^2.

We could compute s and then simplify.

- C and D are on MN, so CD is along MN.

Then the polar line is (-2)(x-2)+7(y+4)=5 → -2x+4+7y+28=5 → -2x+7y+32=5 → -2x+7y+27=0 → 2x - 7y = 27. That's not parallel to AP (slope -2) nor to L (2x+y=5). So not.

Thus not that.

Let's try a different coordinate approach: Place M = (0,0), N = (1,0). Then we have A = (a, h). We also have P = (x_P, y_P). We can compute everything. Then H = (x_P, y_H). Let's denote:

Let A and P be known. Then v = P - A. Then

E = A + λ v
F = A + μ v

with λ, μ as above.

Now, B is the reflection of A across MN, which is horizontal. So B = A - (0, 2h) where (0,2h) is vertical. But note that h is not independent; it's related to A and M,N. However we can also express B in terms of A and maybe something like the vector (0, -2h). Since A = (a, h). So B = (a, -h). So B - A = (0, -2h). So B = A - 2h * (0,1). That's fine.

Now, we can compute the equations for O. Let's write O = (X,Y). Then:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 → (X - a)^2 + (Y + h)^2 = (X - (a + λ dx))^2 + (Y - (h + λ dy))^2. 

Similarly, |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 → (X - a)^2 + (Y + h)^2 = (X - (a + μ dx))^2 + (Y - (h + μ dy))^2.

Let's simplify the difference between the two equations to get O·v. Indeed, subtract the two equations:

|O - E|^2 - |O - F|^2 = 0 → (|O - E|^2 - |O - F|^2) = 0 → (O - E)·(O - E) - (O - F)·(O - F) = 0 → -2 O·E + |E|^2 + 2 O·F - |F|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2. Since F - E = (μ - λ) v. So

2 (μ - λ) O·v = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Thus O·v = (|F|^2 - |E|^2) / (2 (μ - λ)). That's one equation. So we can compute O·v.

Now, the other equation from B and E gives: 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. But we can also use B and F. They are equivalent given the O·v equation. So we have two independent linear equations: one involving O·v, another involving O·(E - B). That's enough to solve for O. However O is a vector; O·v and O·(E - B) are scalars. But we need O itself. Since we have two linear equations, but O has two components. In 2D, two independent linear equations determine O uniquely (if they are independent). However O·v is a linear equation in O, and O·(E - B) is another linear equation. So indeed, we can solve for O. That's a system of two linear equations. That's easier than solving with M_EF.

Let's try that. We'll need to compute E - B and |E|^2 - |B|^2. Then we can solve for O.

So the plan:

So we can treat O as (X,Y). Then we have:

Simplify these to linear equations. Let's do that.

Take the difference between (1) and (2) (they both equal |O-B|^2). Actually we have two equations that both assert that |O-B|^2 equals |O-E|^2 and |O-F|^2. So we can set |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2, which gives a linear equation. That's what we did: O·v = (|F|^2 - |E|^2) / (2 (μ - λ)). So that's one linear equation.

Then we also have |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, which gives another linear equation.

So we can solve these two linear equations.

Let's compute |O-E|^2 - |O-F|^2 = 0 → (O-E)·(O-E) - (O-F)·(O-F) = 0 → -2 O·E + |E|^2 + 2 O·F - |F|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Since F - E = (μ - λ) v. So 2 (μ - λ) O·v = |F|^2 - |E|^2 → O·v = \frac{ |F|^2 - |E|^2 }{2 (μ - λ)}. (EqA)

Now, compute |O-B|^2 - |O-E|^2 = 0 → 2 O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. (EqB)

Thus we have two linear equations.

Now, we can compute |E|^2, |F|^2, |B|^2 in terms of known quantities. Let's do that with general d, r, R. Then we can find O.

Given that we have expressions for A·v and |v|^2, maybe we can compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 and |F|^2 - |E|^2 nicely. Let's attempt.

We have:

|B|^2 = |(a, -h)|^2 = a^2 + h^2 = r^2.

|F|^2 = |A|^2 + 2μ A·v + μ^2 |v|^2.

|v|^2 = \frac{d r (d + R - r)}{R - d + r} + 2 r x_P + r^2.

Let's compute y_P - h using the relation a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. That gives a x_P + h y_P = - r x_P. Then

y_P = \frac{ - r x_P - a x_P }{h} = - \frac{ x_P (r + a) }{h}.

Thus

y_P - h = - \frac{ x_P (r + a) }{h} - h = - \frac{ x_P (r + a) + h^2 }{h}.

So dy = - \frac{N}{h} with N = x_P (r + a) + h^2.

Now, note that h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So

N = x_P (r + a) + r^2 - a^2 = x_P (r + a) + (r - a)(r + a) = (r + a)(x_P + r - a).

Thus N = (r + a)(x_P + r - a). That's a factorization. That's nice! Because we can combine.

Now, x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So

N = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R - r}{2} + r - a ) = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R - r + 2r - 2a}{2} ) = (r + a) ( \frac{d + R + r - 2a}{2} ).

But we also have a expression for a in terms of d, r, R. Maybe we can simplify further.

Compute d + R + r - 2a. Since a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So 2a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / d. Then

d + R + r - 2a = d + R + r - \frac{ d^2 + r^2 - R^2 }{ d } = \frac{ d^2 + dR + dr - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) }{ d } = \frac{ dR + dr - r^2 + R^2 }{ d }.

So

N = (r + a) * \frac{ dR + dr - r^2 + R^2 }{ 2d }.

But that seems messy. However we also have an expression for r + a. Let's compute r + a in terms of d, r, R. We already have r + a = \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{ 2d }.

So

N = \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{ 2d } * \frac{ dR + dr - r^2 + R^2 }{ 2d } = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (dR + dr - r^2 + R^2) }{ 4 d^2 }.

But earlier we derived N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ 4 d^2 }? That was after simplifying with the product we had. Let's check: We had N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ 4 d^2 }. That's a different expression. Let's reconcile.

We have N = x_P (r + a) + h^2. And we expressed h^2 = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2}. And x_P (r + a) = \frac{ (d + R - r) }{2} * \frac{ (d + r)^2 - R^2 }{2 d } = \frac{ (d + R - r) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d}. So

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d} ( d + R - r ) + \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 }.

Factor \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 } times something. Let's combine:

First term: \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) d }{4 d^2}? Actually multiply numerator and denominator by d to get denominator 4 d^2: \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) d }{4 d^2}. That's not simpler. Let's compute common denominator 4 d^2.

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r) }{4 d} + \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2 } = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4} \left( \frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } \right).

Now we already simplified the bracket to \frac{ (R + r)(d + R - r) }{ d^2 }? Let's redo that simplification:

Let’s compute \frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } = \frac{ d (d + R - r) + (R^2 - (d - r)^2) }{ d^2 }.

Now compute numerator: d(d + R - r) = d^2 + dR - dr. And R^2 - (d - r)^2 = R^2 - (d^2 - 2dr + r^2) = R^2 - d^2 + 2dr - r^2. Sum: (d^2 + dR - dr) + (R^2 - d^2 + 2dr - r^2) = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2. So numerator = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2.

Now, note that (d + R - r)(R + r) = (d + R - r)(R + r) = dR + dr + R^2 - r^2. Indeed, compute: (d + R - r)*(R + r) = d(R + r) + (R - r)*(R + r) = dR + dr + (R^2 - r^2). That's exactly the numerator. So numerator = (d + R - r)(R + r). Thus

\frac{ d + R - r }{d} + \frac{ R^2 - (d - r)^2 }{ d^2 } = \frac{ (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ d^2 }.

Therefore

N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4} * \frac{ (d + R - r)(R + r) }{ d^2 }.

So N = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)(R + r) }{4 d^2 }.

That's what we had. Good. So N involves ((d + r)^2 - R^2) = (d + r - R)(d + r + R). That's a product of three factors. So dy = - N / h.

Now, we also have |v|^2 = dx^2 + dy^2. So we could compute |v|^2 using that expression. But maybe we can also compute |v|^2 from the identity:

But we have N expressed. So N^2 / h^2 becomes something like \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2)^2 (d + R - r)^2 (R + r)^2 }{16 d^4 h^2}. And h^2 is \frac{ ((R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2} = \frac{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) }{4 d^2}. So N^2 / h^2 = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2)^2 (d + R - r)^2 (R + r)^2 }{16 d^4} * \frac{4 d^2}{ (R^2 - (d - r)^2) ((d + r)^2 - R^2) } = \frac{ ((d + r)^2 - R^2) (d + R - r)^2 (R + r)^2 }{4 d^2 (R^2 - (d - r)^2)}.

Now, (d + R - r)^2 appears. But note that (R^2 - (d - r)^2) = (R - (d - r))(R + (d - r)) = (R - d + r)(R + d - r). So it's something like. Also, ((d + r)^2 - R^2) = (d + r - R)(d + r + R). So we have many factors. This is messy.

Similarly, |F|^2 - |E|^2 = (|A|^2 + 2μ A·v + μ^2 |v|^2) - (|A|^2 + 2λ A·v + λ^2 |v|^2) = 2(μ - λ) A·v + (μ^2 - λ^2) |v|^2.

|F|^2 - |E|^2 = 2(μ - λ) * ( - r (x_P + r) ) + (μ^2 - λ^2) |v|^2 = -2 r (μ - λ) (x_P + r) + (μ - λ)(μ + λ) |v|^2 = (μ - λ) ( -2 r (x_P + r) + (μ + λ) |v|^2 ).

But we also have an expression for O·v from EqA: O·v = (|F|^2 - |E|^2) / (2 (μ - λ)). That simplifies:

So O·v = \frac{ -2 r (x_P + r) + (μ + λ) |v|^2 }{2}.   (EqA')

Now, also compute O·(λ v + (0,2h)) = \frac{ |E|^2 - |B|^2 }{2}.

Now, we also have from the equation with F (or from the perpendicular bisector of EF) we can get O·v. So we have two linear equations in O·v and O_y. That's sufficient to determine O·v and O_y. However we can also get O_x from the relation O·v after finding Y. That's a linear equation. So we can solve.

But we might be able to find a simpler expression for O_y directly. Let's compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 2λ A·v + λ^2 |v|^2. Since A·v = -r (x_P + r). So

Now, λ = r (d + r + R) / |v|^2. So λ^2 |v|^2 = r^2 (d + r + R)^2 / |v|^2. And λ = r (d + r + R) / |v|^2. So -2 r λ (x_P + r) = -2 r * (r (d + r + R) / |v|^2) * (x_P + r) = -2 r^2 (d + r + R) (x_P + r) / |v|^2.

Thus

Combine over |v|^2: = \frac{ r^2 (d + r + R) }{ |v|^2 } \left( (d + r + R) - 2 (x_P + r) \right).

Now, x_P = (d + R - r)/2. So x_P + r = (d + R - r)/2 + r = (d + R - r + 2r)/2 = (d + R + r)/2. So

2 (x_P + r) = d + R + r.

Thus (d + r + R) - 2 (x_P + r) = (d + r + R) - (d + R + r) = 0. So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 0! That would imply E and B have the same distance from origin? But |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 0 means |E| = |B| = r. However is that true? In numeric, |E|^2 = 2.4^2 + (-1.8)^2 = 5.76 + 3.24 = 9, so |E| = 3, which equals r. So indeed, |E| = r. So E lies on Ω, so |E| = r. So that's consistent. But we already know that E is on Ω, so |E| = r. So indeed, |E|^2 - |B|^2 = r^2 - r^2 = 0. So that's trivial. So EqB becomes 2λ O·v + 4h O_y = 0 → 2λ O·v + 4h O_y = 0 → λ O·v + 2h O_y = 0. Now, O·v is something we can compute. That's EqA'. So we have:

λ O·v + 2h O_y = 0.   (1)

And O·v = \frac{ -2 r (x_P + r) + (μ + λ) |v|^2 }{2}.   (2)

But we also have another equation from |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2, which we used to get O·v. That's EqA. So that's consistent.

Now, we need to solve for O. We have two linear equations: (1) and (2) (where (2) expresses O·v in terms of known quantities). But (2) is not an equation involving O; it's an expression for O·v. However we also have the definition of O·v = X dx + Y dy. So we have:

X dx + Y dy = \frac{ -2 r (x_P + r) + (μ + λ) |v|^2 }{2}.   (EqA)

And λ (X dx + Y dy) + 2h Y = 0 → λ (X dx + Y dy) + 2h Y = 0.   (EqB)
